###begin article-title 0
###xml 4 7 4 7 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 101 104 101 104 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 33 38 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
The CBF gene family in hexaploid wheat and its relationship to the phylogenetic complexity of cereal CBFs
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
Communicated by J.-K. Zhu.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 495 498 495 498 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 567 578 567 578 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. aestivum</italic>
###xml 583 596 583 596 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 629 632 629 632 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 657 660 657 660 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 520 525 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 552 557 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 567 578 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">T. aestivum</span>
###xml 583 596 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
Most temperate plants tolerate both chilling and freezing temperatures whereas many species from tropical regions suffer chilling injury when exposed to temperatures slightly above freezing. Cold acclimation induces the expression of cold-regulated genes needed to protect plants against freezing stress. This induction is mediated, in part, by the CBF transcription factor family. To understand the evolution and function of this family in cereals, we identified and characterized 15 different CBF genes from hexaploid wheat. Our analyses reveal that wheat species, T. aestivum and T. monococcum, may contain up to 25 different CBF genes, and that Poaceae CBFs can be classified into 10 groups that share a common phylogenetic origin and similar structural characteristics. Six of these groups (IIIc, IIId, IVa, IVb, IVc and IVd) are found only in the Pooideae suggesting they represent the CBF response machinery that evolved recently during colonization of temperate habitats. Expression studies reveal that five of the Pooideae-specific groups display higher constitutive and low temperature inducible expression in the winter cultivar, and a diurnal regulation pattern during growth at warm temperature. The higher constitutive and inducible expression within these CBF groups is an inherited trait that may play a predominant role in the superior low temperature tolerance capacity of winter cultivars and possibly be a basis of genetic variability in freezing tolerance within the Pooideae subfamily.
###end p 2

###begin title 5
Keywords
###end title 5
###begin title 6
Introduction
###end title 6
###begin p 7
###xml 123 127 123 127 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">1987</xref>
###xml 179 190 179 190 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 536 539 530 533 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">COR</italic>
###xml 641 645 635 639 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR45">1999</xref>
###xml 662 665 656 659 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">COR</italic>
###xml 915 926 909 920 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 986 995 980 989 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF/DREB1</italic>
###xml 1022 1026 1016 1020 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR43">1997</xref>
###xml 1039 1043 1033 1037 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR31">1998</xref>
###xml 1140 1151 1134 1145 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 1187 1190 1181 1184 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">COR</italic>
###xml 1257 1261 1251 1255 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR24">1998</xref>
###xml 1274 1278 1268 1272 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR31">1998</xref>
###xml 1501 1505 1495 1499 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">2001</xref>
###xml 161 166 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 306 312 <span type="species:ncbi:4081">tomato</span>
###xml 314 319 <span type="species:ncbi:4577">maize</span>
###xml 324 328 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">rice</span>
The ability of plants to respond and adapt to low temperature (LT) stresses varies greatly with species (Sakai and Larcher 1987). Most temperate plants, such as wheat, canola and Arabidopsis, are able to tolerate both chilling and freezing temperatures. In contrast, species from tropical regions, such as tomato, maize and rice are unable to tolerate freezing temperatures and even suffer chilling injury when exposed to temperatures in the range of 0-12degreesC. The cold acclimation process induces the expression of cold-regulated (COR) genes, whose products are thought to be necessary for protection against freezing stress (Thomashow 1999). Many of these COR genes contain copies of the C-repeat/dehydration-responsive element (CRT/DRE) in their promoters, which has the core motif CCGAC and is responsible for the LT-responsiveness of these genes. The factors that bind the CRT/DRE were first identified in Arabidopsis and designated CRT-Binding Factors/DRE-binding proteins 1 (CBF/DREB1) genes (Stockinger et al. 1997; Liu et al. 1998). These genes encode LT-induced transcription factors that when constitutively overexpressed in Arabidopsis mimic cold acclimation by inducing COR gene expression and freezing tolerance (FT) (Jaglo-Ottosen et al. 1998; Liu et al. 1998). CBF-like proteins have been isolated from a wide range of plants that include species capable and incapable of cold acclimation, suggesting that the CBF cold response pathway is broadly conserved in plants (Jaglo et al. 2001).
###end p 7
###begin p 8
###xml 151 162 151 162 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 334 338 334 338 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">2006</xref>
###xml 371 382 371 382 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 942 946 942 946 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">2005</xref>
###xml 963 967 963 967 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 1023 1034 1023 1034 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 1112 1116 1112 1116 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">2006</xref>
###xml 1296 1307 1296 1307 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 1332 1343 1332 1343 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 1379 1383 1379 1383 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF1</italic>
###xml 1385 1386 1385 1386 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</italic>
###xml 1392 1393 1392 1393 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">3</italic>
###xml 1503 1507 1503 1507 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">2004</xref>
###xml 1524 1528 1524 1528 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">2004</xref>
###xml 1556 1560 1556 1560 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR47">2006</xref>
###xml 1563 1567 1563 1567 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF4</italic>
###xml 1616 1620 1616 1620 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR16">2002</xref>
###xml 1627 1631 1627 1631 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DDF1</italic>
###xml 1636 1640 1636 1640 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DDF2</italic>
###xml 1723 1727 1723 1727 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR33">2004</xref>
###xml 1051 1055 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">rice</span>
###xml 1312 1316 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">rice</span>
The CBF/DREB1 proteins belong to the AP2/ERF superfamily of DNA-binding proteins. This protein family has in common the AP2 DNA-binding motif. The 122 Arabidopsis members of the ERF family containing one AP2 DNA-binding motif were divided into 12 groups, with several of the groups being further divided into subgroups (Nakano et al. 2006). The six CBF/DREB1 proteins of Arabidopsis were included in subgroup IIIc, one of the five group III subgroups. The CBF proteins are distinguished from other group III members by a conserved set of amino acid sequences (motif CMIII-3) flanking the AP2 DNA-binding domain. Other motifs found in CBF proteins (CMIII-1, CMIII-2 and CMIII-4) are also present in one or more of the group III subgroups, suggesting related molecular functions may be conserved between subgroups. These features were previously noted as conserved features of the C-terminal activation domain between aligned CBFs (Wang et al. 2005; Skinner et al. 2005). Comparison of group III proteins between the eudicot Arabidopsis and the monocot rice reveals they share four common subgroups (Nakano et al. 2006) suggesting a functional diversification of group III proteins had occurred before the divergence of these two species. The 4 ancestral genes have amplified to 23 and 26 genes in Arabidopsis and rice, respectively. Arabidopsis CBF studies have demonstrated that CBF1, 2, and 3 function in the cold-acclimation pathway with redundant and some possibly specific functions (Gilmour et al. 2004; Novillo et al. 2004; Van Buskirk and Thomashow 2006), CBF4 is involved in drought adaptation (Haake et al. 2002), and DDF1 and DDF2 are involved in gibberellin biosynthesis and salt stress tolerance (Magome et al. 2004). Based on these findings, the functional divergence of group III ERF proteins in monocots may differ from eudicots and therefore warrant a characterization of monocot genes.
###end p 8
###begin p 9
###xml 392 396 392 396 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR14">2004</xref>
###xml 459 462 459 462 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 653 656 653 656 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 788 792 788 792 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR26">2001</xref>
###xml 1382 1387 1382 1387 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">2002a</xref>
###xml 1644 1649 1644 1649 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR22">2002b</xref>
###xml 1716 1719 1716 1719 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1849 1852 1849 1852 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1998 2002 1998 2002 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR14">2004</xref>
###xml 2057 2070 2057 2070 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 2086 2090 2086 2090 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 2116 2120 2116 2120 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">2006</xref>
###xml 2155 2159 2155 2159 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2007</xref>
###xml 2217 2220 2217 2220 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 2348 2351 2348 2351 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 94 99 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 101 106 <span type="species:ncbi:4577">maize</span>
###xml 111 115 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">rice</span>
###xml 219 224 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 822 826 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">rice</span>
###xml 845 850 <span type="species:ncbi:4577">maize</span>
###xml 972 977 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 979 985 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 990 993 <span type="species:ncbi:4498">oat</span>
###xml 1057 1060 <span type="species:ncbi:4498">oat</span>
###xml 1112 1118 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 1123 1128 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1170 1173 <span type="species:ncbi:4550">rye</span>
###xml 1299 1305 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 1310 1313 <span type="species:ncbi:4550">rye</span>
###xml 1328 1333 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1430 1441 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">bread wheat</span>
###xml 1618 1629 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">bread wheat</span>
###xml 1652 1657 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">Wheat</span>
###xml 1907 1912 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 2057 2070 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 2093 2099 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 2136 2141 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
Members of the Poaceae have been targeted for study since they contain the major cereal crops wheat, maize and rice which provide >60% of the calories and proteins for our daily life. To meet the needs of the projected human population by 2050, cereal grain production must increase at an annual rate of 2% on an area of land that will not increase much beyond the present level (Gill et al. 2004). Therefore, significant advances in our understanding of the CBF family in cereals are essential to develop needed strategies to protect crops from losses caused by abiotic stress. The Poaceae represent an excellent model system to study the roles of the CBF family in the evolution of LT tolerance. The Poaceae radiated some 55-70 million years ago (MYA) into several subfamilies (Kellogg 2001). The subfamilies Oryzaceae (rice) and Panicoideae (maize) have a more tropical geographical distribution compared to members of the Pooideae, which contain the temperate cereals wheat, barley and oat. LT tolerance within the Pooideae subfamily ranges from low in oat (a Poeae tribe representative), to intermediary in barley and wheat (Triticeae tribe), to highly tolerant in rye (Triticeae tribe). The estimated divergence time between the Triticeae and Poeae is around 35 MYA, and within the Triticeae, barley and rye diverged from wheat around 11 and 7 MYA, respectively (Huang et al. 2002a). The more recent evolutionary history of bread wheat started with an adaptive radiation of the diploid progenitors around 2.2-4.5 MYA followed by successive hybridizations around <0.5 MYA and 8,000 years ago to produce hexaploid bread wheat (Huang et al. 2002b). Wheat is an interesting model since the comparative analysis of CBF gene function among closer and more distantly related species may shed light on important evolutionary trends that have sculpted CBF function. Furthermore, the three genomes of hexaploid wheat are known to contain differences for many agronomically important genes (Gill et al. 2004), and recently, a LT tolerance QTL on chromosome 5 of T. monococcum (Miller et al. 2006), barley (Skinner et al. 2006) and hexaploid wheat (Baga et al. 2007) was found to coincide with the location of 11, 12 and 2 CBF genes, respectively. The exact molecular explanation for this LT tolerance QTL is not known but indicates the possibility that CBF genes may be at the base of this important trait.
###end p 9
###begin p 10
###xml 5 8 5 8 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 83 87 83 87 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">2001</xref>
###xml 112 116 112 116 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">2003</xref>
###xml 133 137 133 137 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 160 164 160 164 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR6">2002</xref>
###xml 170 174 170 174 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR51">2002</xref>
###xml 191 195 191 195 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">2004</xref>
###xml 212 216 212 216 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 239 243 239 243 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">2001</xref>
###xml 257 261 257 261 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">2005</xref>
###xml 278 282 278 282 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 302 306 302 306 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">2005</xref>
###xml 322 326 322 326 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 333 352 333 352 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Festuca arundinacea</italic>
###xml 366 370 366 370 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">2005</xref>
###xml 427 431 427 431 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 452 456 452 456 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 489 492 489 492 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 680 683 680 683 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 778 781 778 781 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 806 809 806 809 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 951 954 951 954 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 65 68 <span type="species:ncbi:4550">rye</span>
###xml 90 94 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">rice</span>
###xml 140 146 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 219 224 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 333 352 <span type="species:ncbi:4606">Festuca arundinacea</span>
###xml 705 710 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 741 746 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
Many CBF genes have been identified from Poaceae species such as rye (Jaglo et al. 2001), rice (Dubouzet et al. 2003; Skinner et al. 2005), barley (Choi et al. 2002; Xue 2002; Francia et al. 2004; Skinner et al. 2005), wheat (Jaglo et al. 2001; Kume et al. 2005; Skinner et al. 2005; Vagujfalvi et al. 2005; Miller et al. 2006), and Festuca arundinacea (Tang et al. 2005). These studies, in particular those of Skinner et al. (2005) and Miller et al. (2006), have revealed that the cereal CBF family is large and complex. To better understand the functions of this gene family during cold stress and its evolution in the Poaceae, we initiated a study to identify and characterize CBF genes from hexaploid wheat. Here, we show that hexaploid wheat contains at least 15 different CBF genes, and that Poaceae CBFs can be subdivided into groups with specific characteristics. These findings expand our understanding on the functional categories of cereal CBF genes and provide a starting point for future studies.
###end p 10
###begin title 11
Materials and methods
###end title 11
###begin title 12
Preparation of the cDNA libraries
###end title 12
###begin p 13
###xml 44 61 44 61 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Triticum aestivum</italic>
###xml 114 117 114 117 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 271 275 271 275 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR19">2006</xref>
###xml 896 899 896 899 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Sal</italic>
###xml 933 936 933 936 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Not</italic>
###xml 965 967 965 967 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">15</sub>
###xml 1107 1110 1107 1110 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Not</italic>
###xml 44 61 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">Triticum aestivum</span>
###xml 108 113 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 449 454 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 533 538 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 582 587 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 694 699 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 734 739 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
Five different cDNA libraries prepared from Triticum aestivum L. cv Norstar were used to identify expressed wheat CBF genes in this study (Table S1). Plant growth conditions, RNA purification and cDNA library construction were described in detail elsewhere (Houde et al. 2006). Briefly the five libraries (L2-L6) were prepared from the following pooled mRNA populations: (L2) aerial parts (leaf and crown) from control and long-term cold acclimated wheat (1-53 days); (L3) root tissue from control, cold-acclimated and salt stressed wheat; (L4) aerial parts of dehydration stressed wheat; (L5) crown tissue during vernalization and different developmental stages of spike and seed formation in wheat; (L6) crown and leaf tissues from wheat after short exposures to LT in the light and in the dark. All cDNAs synthesized were directionally cloned into the pCMV.SPORT6 vector (Invitrogen) with the SalI adaptor (GTCGACCCACGCGTCCG) and NotI primer adaptor (GCGGCCGCCCT15). For the last four libraries, the first strand cDNA reaction mix contained methylated dCTP to prevent cDNAs from internal cleavage by the NotI restriction enzyme used for directional cloning. For the last library, the 'GeneRacer' kit (Invitrogen) was used prior to first strand synthesis to produce a library containing a high proportion (95%) of full-length cDNAs. For each library, six million primary transformants were obtained, amplified and frozen as glycerol stocks.
###end p 13
###begin p 14
###xml 86 87 85 86 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">6</sup>
To prepare plasmids for PCR experiments, 40 mul of a bacterial library stock (>40 x 106 clones) were inoculated into LB media (100 ml) supplemented with ampicillin and grown at 30degreesC for 6-8 h. Plasmids were isolated using the QIAprep Miniprep system (QIAGEN) and the quantity was evaluated on a gel.
###end p 14
###begin title 15
###xml 24 27 24 27 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 18 23 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
Identification of wheat CBF genes
###end title 15
###begin p 16
###xml 92 93 92 93 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 264 268 264 268 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">2001</xref>
###xml 353 357 353 357 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR20">1999</xref>
###xml 408 411 408 411 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 727 730 727 730 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 870 873 870 873 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 960 963 960 963 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1089 1093 1089 1093 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR39">2000</xref>
###xml 1470 1473 1470 1473 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1671 1674 1669 1672 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Pfx</italic>
###xml 2167 2170 2147 2150 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 2293 2294 2273 2274 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 2418 2425 2398 2405 <label xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Table&#160;1</label>

###xml 225 230 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 503 508 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 796 801 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 983 988 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 2461 2466 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 4137 4142 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 4580 4593 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 4619 4625 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
The gene cloning approach, gene names and GenBank accession numbers are summarized in Table 1. To initiate this project, available CBF protein sequences were used for data mining of the NCBI NR and EST databases in search of wheat homologs. The mRNA (Jaglo et al. 2001) and EST sequences were assembled into virtual mRNAs using CAP3 () (Huang and Madan 1999). This initial assembly was updated as additional CBF genes were sequenced from the project. Virtual mRNAs that contained EST sequences from the wheat genomics of abiotic stress (WGAS) project () were ordered and completely sequenced at the Genome Quebec sequencing center (McGill University, Canada). Initially, hybridization probes corresponding to either incomplete CBF genes or to their AP2 DNA binding region were used to screen the wheat plasmid cDNA libraries L3 and L6 to identify additional full length CBF clones. Because this approach was time consuming, a PCR strategy was used to identify CBF genes expressed in wheat. From one to three gene specific primers (GSPs) were designed using Primer3 () (Rozen and Skaletsky 2000) in the forward (5' mRNA region) or reverse (3' mRNA region) directions on the assembled virtual mRNAs. In order to drive the PCR reaction, the GSPs were designed with a higher Tm than the universal primers M13F 5'-AGATCCCAAGCTAGCAGTTTTCCCAGTCACGA-3' and M13R 5'-GAGCGGATAACAATTTCACACAGGAAACAGCTATGA-3' found in the pCMV.SPORT6 vector. A list of GSPs used in the isolation of CBF genes is given in Table S2. Amplification was performed using a GSP (0.6 muM) and the corresponding universal primer (0.3 muM), 10 ng of plasmid DNA from a library, 1x or 2x enhancer solution, and Pfx DNA polymerase (Invitrogen) following the manufacturer's recommendations. Briefly, the PCR thermal-cycling parameters were initiated with a progressive step down in annealing temperature that ended with 45 cycles of 94degreesC for 20 s, 64degreesC for 20 s and 68degreesC for 150 s. PCR products were analyzed on a gel and lanes that produced DNA fragments of expected size were chosen for subcloning using the Zero Blunt TOPO PCR Cloning Kit (Invitrogen) and then sent for sequencing. Novel CBF sequences were fully sequenced and overlapping sequences were merged to produce the longest possible gene sequence (Table 1). Several clones from independent amplification reactions were sequenced in order to correct errors introduced during PCR. ###begin title 24
Expression profiling using northern analysis
###end title 24
###begin p 25
###xml 26 43 26 43 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Triticum aestivum</italic>
###xml 265 267 258 260 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;2</sup>
###xml 269 271 262 264 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;1</sup>
###xml 598 599 585 586 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>
###xml 676 680 663 667 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">1990</xref>
###xml 827 829 813 815 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">32</sup>
###xml 931 937 917 923 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFs</italic>
###xml 11 16 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 26 43 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">Triticum aestivum</span>
###xml 630 635 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
The spring wheat cultivar Triticum aestivum L. cv Quantum and the winter cultivar Norstar were germinated in moist sterilized vermiculite in a growth chamber (Model-E15, Conviron) for 7 days at 20degreesC and 70% relative humidity under an irradiance of 250 mumol m-2 s-1 and a 16-h photoperiod. At the end of this period, 5 g of control leaves were sampled and individually frozen. A cold treatment (4degreesC) was initiated by changing the temperature in the growth chamber 4 h into the day phase and continued under the same irradiance and photoperiod conditions for the times indicated in Fig. 4. Total RNA was extracted from wheat leaves as described (Danyluk and Sarhan 1990), and equal amounts (10 mug) were separated on formaldehyde-agarose gels. Transfers to positively charged nylon membranes and hybridizations with 32P-labeled probes were performed using standard molecular biology techniques. Probes for the different TaCBFs were designed outside of the AP2 domain to avoid cross-hybridization with known CBF transcripts (Table S3). All filters were washed at high stringency (0.1x SSC, 0.1% SDS; 65degreesC) for 30 min. Membranes were exposed to BioMax-MS films (Kodak) and Molecular Imager FX screens (Bio-Rad).
###end p 25
###begin title 26
Expression profiling by quantitative real-time PCR
###end title 26
###begin title 27
Plant growth conditions and cDNA synthesis
###end title 27
###begin p 28
###xml 213 215 206 208 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;2</sup>
###xml 217 219 210 212 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;1</sup>
Spring cultivar Manitou and winter cultivar Norstar were germinated in a mixture of 50% black earth and 50% Pro-Mix (Premier) for 8 days at 20degreesC under a 16-h photoperiod with a light intensity of 250 mumol m-2 s-1. In this experiment, Manitou was used as the spring cultivar in order to compare the response to the Quantum spring cultivar. At the end of this period, 8 control seedlings were sampled every 2 h on dry ice, and immediately frozen at -70degreesC. Cold treatment (4degreesC) was initiated by changing the temperature in the growth chamber 4 h before the night cycle, and sampling as indicated in each Figure. Sampling of control and cold-treated plants every 2 h was chosen so that closely spaced time points act as closely related biological replicates in addition to providing a better resolution of the expression characteristics of a gene. Night samples were collected by opening the growth chamber in the dark, removing plants to another room and harvesting rapidly. Total RNA was isolated using the RNeasy Plant Mini Kit (QIAGEN) using the optional on-column DNAse digestion. Purified RNA (2.8 mug) was reverse transcribed in a 20 mul reaction volume using the Superscript II first strand cDNA synthesis system for RT-PCR (Invitrogen). Parallel reactions for each RNA sample were run in the absence of Superscript II (no RT control) to assess for genomic DNA contamination. The reaction was terminated by heat inactivation; the cDNA product was treated with RNase H, and diluted in water (20 ng/mul) for storage (-20degreesC).
###end p 28
###begin title 29
Design of gene-specific primers
###end title 29
###begin p 30
###xml 100 105 100 105 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
###xml 24 29 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
The genome of hexaploid wheat contains three genomes inherited from three diploid ancestors. The 37 TaCBF gene sequences identified in this study were analyzed using ClustalW () and phylogenetic characterization. This analysis revealed 15 groups of genes containing one to three homeologous copies in each group. Primers were specifically designed to monitor the expression of only one representative gene per group. This representative was chosen randomly. Fluorescent TaqMan-MGB probes as well as the non-fluorescent primers (Table S4) were designed using the combination of Primer Express Software Version 2.0 (Applied Biosystems) and Primer3. BLASTN searches against EST and NR databases were performed to confirm the gene specificity of the primers. Non-fluorescent primers were synthesized by Invitrogen and TaqMan-MGB probes by Applied Biosystems.
###end p 30
###begin title 31
PCR amplification and data analysis
###end title 31
###begin p 32
Quantitative real-time PCR assays for each gene target were performed in quadruplicate on an ABI Prism 7000 sequence detection system (Applied Biosystems) using the eukaryotic 18S rRNA as the endogenous control (Applied Biosystems #4319413E). From the diluted cDNA, 2 mul (40 ng) was used as a template in a 25-mul PCR reaction containing 1x TaqMan universal PCR master mix (Invitrogen), 0.9 muM of non-fluorescent primers, and 0.25 muM of TaqMan-MGB fluorescent probe. The PCR thermal cycling parameters were 50degreesC for 2 min, 95degreesC for 10 min followed by 50 cycles of 95degreesC for 15 s and 60degreesC for 1 min.
###end p 32
###begin p 33
###xml 103 116 103 108 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#8722;&#916;&#916;Ct</sup>
###xml 157 160 149 152 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">min</sub>
###xml 163 166 155 158 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 211 215 203 207 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR32">2001</xref>
###xml 267 270 259 262 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>
###xml 287 290 279 282 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">min</sub>
All calculations and statistical analysis were performed by the SDS RQ Manager 1.1 software using the 2-DeltaDeltaCt method with a relative quantification RQmin/RQmax confidence set at 95% (Livak and Schmittgen 2001). The error bars display the calculated maximum (RQmax) and minimum (RQmin) expression levels that represent SE of the mean expression level (RQ value). Collectively, the upper and lower limits define the region of expression within which the true expression level value is likely to occur (SDS RQ Manager 1.1 software user manual; Applied Biosystems). Amplification efficiency (90-100%) for the 15 primer sets was determined by amplification of cDNA dilution series using 80, 20, 10, 5, 2.5, and 1.25 ng per reaction (data not shown). Specificity of the RT-PCR products was assessed by gel electrophoresis.
###end p 33
###begin title 34
###xml 27 32 27 32 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
Chromosome localization of TaCBF genes
###end title 34
###begin p 35
###xml 55 59 55 59 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">1997</xref>
###xml 525 529 525 529 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">1988</xref>
###xml 545 549 545 549 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">1996</xref>
###xml 597 617 597 617 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Aegilops cylindrical</italic>
###xml 1175 1180 1147 1152 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
###xml 88 93 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
Genomic DNA was extracted and quantified (Limin et al. 1997) from several stocks of the wheat cultivar Chinese Spring: ditelocentric series provided by the USDA from E. R. Sears collection (all chromosomes are present but in each line one chromosome pair is represented by only the telocentric chromosomes of one arm); chromosome 5 nullisomic-tetrasomic lines (a pair of chromosomes is removed and replaced by another pair of homoeologous chromosomes); and deletion lines for homoeologous group 5AL and 5DL chromosomes (Endo 1988; Endo and Gill 1996) generated using the gametocidal chromosome of Aegilops cylindrical. From the diluted genomic stocks, 2 mul (20 ng) was used as a template in a 25-mul PCR reaction containing 1x TaqMan universal PCR master mix (Invitrogen), 0.9 muM non-fluorescent primers, and 0.25 muM TaqMan-MGB fluorescent probe. The PCR thermal cycling parameters were 50degreesC for 2 min, 95degreesC for 10 min followed by 50 cycles of 95degreesC for 15 s and 60degreesC for 1 min. At the end of the run, the Ct values were compared, and genetic stocks that showed a delayed or undetectable amplification were identified as the location of the assayed TaCBF gene.
###end p 35

proposed gene names are tentative since they are based on partial EST sequences</p></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap>
###xml 3922 3929 <span type="species:ncbi:4558">Sorghum</span>
Monocotyledon CBF homologs were identified using the TaCBF nucleotide and protein sequences as queries against the GenBank NR and EST databases. Overlapping ESTs were assembled into virtual cDNAs using CAP3 () (Huang and Madan 1999) and a consensus cDNA sequence was deduced. EST-derived sequences for analyses were obtained by trimming edges that corresponded to low quality error prone regions which were revealed through blastx searches against the NR database. Accession numbers for genes and ESTs used in this study are in Table 2. FASTA files of nucleotide and protein sequences used in this analysis are presented in Supplemental Tables S5 and S6. The degree of sequence identity was determined using ALIGN and FASTA on the Biology Workbench (). Sequences were aligned using ClustalW from the Biology Workbench or from the MEGA software version 3.1 (Kumar et al. 2004), and alignments were refined manually. The MEGA software was used for phylogenetic analyses and the Minimum Evolution tree was derived from this alignment using the Kimura 2-parameter with bootstrap test and default parameters. ###begin p 41
###xml 53 57 53 57 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">1987</xref>
###xml 76 80 76 80 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">1997</xref>
###xml 848 853 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
Hydrophobic cluster analysis (HCA) (Gaboriaud et al. 1987; Callebaut et al. 1997) was conducted using the web-based interface at: (). Briefly, the protein sequences are displayed on a duplicated alpha-helical net in which hydrophobic amino acids (V, I, L, F, M, Y, W) are contoured. Hydrophobic residues separated by four or more nonhydrophobic residues, or a Proline, are placed into distinct clusters. The defined hydrophobic clusters were shown to mainly correspond to the internal faces of regular secondary structures (alpha-helices or beta-strands). Two other amino acids were chosen to be highlighted in this study: proline which confers the greatest constraint to the polypeptide chain and glycine which confers the largest freedom to the chain. This secondary structure information was highlighted on a ClustalW alignment of group-related wheat CBFs.
###end p 41

Identification of wheat CBF genes

###begin p 44
###xml 0 3 0 3 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 96 99 96 99 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 188 191 188 191 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 307 310 307 310 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 331 335 331 335 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">2001</xref>
###xml 349 353 349 353 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">2005</xref>
###xml 370 374 370 374 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 415 418 415 418 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 542 545 542 545 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 784 789 784 789 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
###xml 824 825 824 825 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 866 876 866 876 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">H. vulgare</italic>
###xml 881 894 881 894 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 924 928 924 928 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 944 948 944 948 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 1186 1197 1186 1197 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIa-A11</italic>
###xml 1243 1248 1243 1248 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBF</italic>
###xml 1248 1250 1248 1250 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">11</underline>
###xml 1248 1250 1248 1250 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><underline>11</underline></italic>
###xml 1289 1293 1289 1293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 1501 1512 1501 1512 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. aestivum</italic>
###xml 1517 1530 1517 1530 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 1556 1559 1556 1559 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1983 1986 1983 1986 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 2005 2015 2005 2015 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIIa-</italic>
###xml 2015 2016 2015 2016 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">D</underline>
###xml 2015 2016 2015 2016 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><underline>D</underline></italic>
###xml 2016 2017 2016 2017 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">6</italic>
###xml 2124 2127 2124 2127 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 2201 2212 2201 2212 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIIa-6</italic>
###xml 2212 2214 2212 2214 <underline xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">.1</underline>
###xml 2212 2214 2212 2214 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><underline>.1</underline></italic>
###xml 277 282 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 456 461 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 811 816 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 866 876 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">H. vulgare</span>
###xml 881 894 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 1012 1017 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1164 1169 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1364 1369 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1501 1512 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">T. aestivum</span>
###xml 1517 1530 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 1550 1555 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1929 1934 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
CBF family members are important regulators of FT in plants. Data mining and analyses of cereal CBF sequences present in GenBank suggest that different species contain diverse and complex CBF families. Based on preliminary studies conducted in a number of varieties, hexaploid wheat contains at least seven CBF genes (Jaglo et al. 2001; Kume et al. 2005; Skinner et al. 2005). To maximize our chance of discovering CBF genes involved in the development of wheat FT, several cDNA libraries were constructed (Table S1) and screened to identify CBF genes expressed under various cold acclimation time points and conditions in the freezing tolerant cultivar Norstar. A combination of EST sequencing, cDNA library screening and PCR amplification allowed the identification of 37 expressed TaCBF genes from hexaploid wheat (Table 1). To be consistent with the established H. vulgare and T. monococcum nomenclature (Skinner et al. 2005; Miller et al. 2006), we assigned identical gene numbers to orthologs of hexaploid wheat (2-15) and new consecutive numbers (starting from 19 to 22) to novel genes identified following homology comparison with published and identified wheat sequences (e.g. TaCBFIa-A11 shows the highest homology with its ortholog HvCBF11 identified previously (Skinner et al. 2005). These analyses revealed that the 37 genes identified from hexaploid wheat can be classified into at least 15 different orthologous gene groups with 1-3 homeologous copies in each. Phylogenetic analysis of T. aestivum and T. monococcum genes reveals that wheat CBF genes can be divided into 10 monophyletic groups. Therefore, we included in the proposed CBF nomenclature CBF subgroup information (e.g. CBFIa, II, IIIa, IIIb, IIIc, IIId, IVa, IVb, IVc and IVd) which should facilitate future comparison of monocot CBF properties and functions. In the cases where homeologous copies were mapped to one of the three genomes of hexaploid wheat, a letter designating its location precedes the CBF gene number (e.g. TaCBFIIIa-D6). On the other hand, when the genomic localization of a homeologous copy has not yet been determined, the CBF gene number is followed by a temporary designation of .1, .2 or .3 (e.g. TaCBFIIIa-6.1).
###end p 44
###begin p 45
###xml 28 40 28 40 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIId-19</italic>
###xml 42 53 42 53 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVb-20</italic>
###xml 58 69 58 69 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVd-22</italic>
###xml 239 240 239 240 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 259 268 259 268 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFII-5</italic>
###xml 305 306 305 306 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 322 335 322 335 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 388 392 388 392 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 468 472 468 472 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">2006</xref>
###xml 622 623 622 623 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 821 825 821 825 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">2004</xref>
###xml 839 843 839 843 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2007</xref>
###xml 859 863 859 863 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 880 884 880 884 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">2006</xref>
###xml 952 964 952 964 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIId-19</italic>
###xml 966 977 966 977 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVb-20</italic>
###xml 979 990 979 990 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVb-21</italic>
###xml 995 1006 995 1006 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVd-22</italic>
###xml 1015 1016 1015 1016 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 1062 1065 1062 1065 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1075 1088 1075 1088 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 1104 1108 1104 1108 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 1124 1127 1124 1127 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1160 1164 1160 1164 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR42">2006</xref>
###xml 118 123 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 322 335 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 402 408 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 1075 1088 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 1137 1143 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
At least three gene groups (TaCBFIIId-19, TaCBFIVb-20 and TaCBFIVd-22) represent true orthologous series in hexaploid wheat since the homeologous copies (A, B and D) were identified and mapped to each genome equivalent in our study (Table 1). One gene group (TaCBFII-5) was not mapped in our study (Table 1). However, the T. monococcum ortholog was mapped to chromosome 7A (Miller et al. 2006) and the barley ortholog was mapped to the short arm of 7H (Skinner et al. 2006) suggesting that this gene group will be located on chromosome 7. The vast majority of gene groups (13 out of 15) were mapped to chromosome 5 (Table 1), and at least 9 of these 13 have so far been mapped more precisely to a region, between two deletion breakpoints, associated with a cold tolerance QTL in several Triticeae species (Francia et al. 2004; Baga et al. 2007; Miller et al. 2006; Skinner et al. 2006). The present study allowed the localization of 4 new gene groups (TaCBFIIId-19, TaCBFIVb-20, TaCBFIVb-21 and TaCBFIVd-22) (Table 1) to this QTL region containing the 11 tandem CBF genes in T. monococcum (Miller et al. 2006) and 12 tandem CBF genes in barley (Skinner et al. 2006).
###end p 45
###begin p 46
###xml 152 155 152 155 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 299 312 299 312 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 328 332 328 332 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 338 349 338 349 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. aestivum</italic>
###xml 349 352 349 352 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 465 476 465 476 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. aestivum</italic>
###xml 685 694 685 694 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFII-5</italic>
###xml 696 707 696 707 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIIa-6</italic>
###xml 709 720 709 720 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIIc-3</italic>
###xml 722 732 722 732 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVd-9</italic>
###xml 737 748 737 748 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIVd-22</italic>
###xml 899 908 899 908 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFII-5</italic>
###xml 913 924 913 924 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIIa-6</italic>
###xml 1024 1033 1024 1033 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFII-5</italic>
###xml 1038 1049 1038 1049 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBFIIIa-6</italic>
###xml 299 312 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">T. monococcum</span>
###xml 465 476 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">T. aestivum</span>
A cut-off of 95% was chosen to differentiate between homeologous copies and possible recently duplicated genes. Because homeologous copies of different CBF gene groups would have started diverging around the same time, one would expect them to show similarities in the same range. The comparison of T. monococcum (Miller et al. 2006) and T. aestivumCBF genes (this study) does reveal five orthologous genes with high levels of identity (more than 98%). From the 13 T. aestivum gene groups that contain homeologous copies, only 5 show identities below 95% (the percent identity excludes bases involved in transitions, transversions and gaps) between the homeologous ORFs. These include TaCBFII-5, TaCBFIIIa-6, TaCBFIIIc-3, TaCBFIVd-9 and TaCBFIVd-22 which show identities of 85.9, 91.7, 94.5, 94.8 and 89.9%, respectively. When the identity comparison is repeated with gap regions not included, only TaCBFII-5 and TaCBFIIIa-6 show lower similarities of 93.4 and 93.5%, respectively. Therefore, these results suggest that the TaCBFII-5 and TaCBFIIIa-6 homeologous groups may contain closely related paralogs and/or have diverged at a different rate than other groups.
###end p 46
###begin title 47
###xml 21 24 21 24 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
Phylogeny of monocot CBF genes
###end title 47
###begin p 48
###xml 20 23 20 23 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 88 92 88 92 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR8">2003</xref>
###xml 105 109 105 109 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR38">2004</xref>
###xml 128 132 128 132 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR4">2005</xref>
###xml 148 152 148 152 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR50">2006</xref>
###xml 184 187 184 187 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 288 293 288 293 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
###xml 313 316 313 316 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 404 405 404 405 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 440 443 440 443 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 798 801 798 801 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 936 939 936 939 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
Monocot and eudicot CBF sequences are separated on a phylogenetic tree (Dubouzet et al. 2003; Qin et al. 2004; Brautigam et al. 2005; Xiong and Fei 2006) suggesting that at least some CBF gene function specialization has evolved recently in plants. To understand the relationship between TaCBFs and other monocot CBFs, we searched NR and EST databases and compiled a set of sequences for analysis (Table 2). During our BLAST comparisons of CBFs, we found that it would be difficult to align the entire ORF of distant members with complete confidence. Therefore, the nucleotide sequence of the AP2 DNA binding domain and adjacent CBF signatures were chosen for alignment and phylogeny analysis since these domains are extremely well conserved in the CBF family. To simplify the future comparison of CBF gene functional studies with different monocotyledon plants, we propose a nomenclature that reflects the evolutionary relationship of CBF genes. This will help to distinguish their specific functional roles which may have appeared during their evolution.
###end p 48
###begin p 49
###xml 52 55 52 55 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 114 119 114 119 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
###xml 225 226 225 226 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 249 262 249 262 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">T. monococcum</italic>
###xml 262 265 262 265 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 287 291 287 291 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 426 431 426 431 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TaCBF</italic>
###xml 471 480 471 480 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFII-5</italic>
###xml 490 502 490 502 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIIIb-18</italic>
###xml 510 522 510 522 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIIIc-10</italic>
###xml 530 542 530 542 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIIIc-13</italic>
###xml 552 564 552 564 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIIId-16</italic>
###xml 574 586 574 586 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIIId-17</italic>
###xml 596 606 596 606 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIVa-2</italic>
###xml 619 629 619 629 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBFIVd-4</italic>
###xml 717 720 717 720 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 745 748 745 748 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 826 831 826 831 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBF</italic>
###xml 854 855 854 855 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 873 877 873 877 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 912 924 912 924 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBFIIIc-8B</italic>
###xml 929 941 929 941 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBFIIIc-8C</italic>
###xml 972 985 972 985 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBFIIIc-10B</italic>
###xml 995 1006 995 1006 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBFIVa-2B</italic>
###xml 1016 1027 1016 1027 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBFIVd-4B</italic>
###xml 1040 1051 1040 1051 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBFIVd-4D</italic>
###xml 1244 1247 1244 1247 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1343 1344 1343 1344 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 1360 1363 1360 1363 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">CBF</italic>
###xml 1482 1483 1482 1483 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab2" ref-type="table">2</xref>
###xml 1486 1492 1486 1492 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">HvCBF7</italic>
###xml 1497 1503 1497 1503 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TmCBF7</italic>
###xml 1520 1524 1520 1524 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2005</xref>
###xml 1540 1544 1540 1544 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2006</xref>
###xml 1744 1748 1744 1748 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">2006</xref>
###xml 65 70 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 711 716 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 739 744 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 793 799 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
###xml 1212 1217 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">wheat</span>
###xml 1222 1228 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">barley</span>
To establish the relationship between the different CBF genes in wheat, only one representative of each of the 15 TaCBF gene groups (preferentially the homeologous A copy) was included in this analysis. In addition, 8 (Table 2) of the 13 identified T. monococcumCBF genes (Miller et al. 2006) were included in the analysis since they showed less than 95% identity (gap regions not included in the analysis) with any of the 15 TaCBF gene groups. These lower identities of TmCBFII-5 (86.5%), TmCBFIIIb-18 (76%), TmCBFIIIc-10 (94%), TmCBFIIIc-13 (86.7%), TmCBFIIId-16 (82.1%), TmCBFIIId-17 (78.8%), TmCBFIVa-2 (92.4%) and TmCBFIVd-4 (87.1%) with their closest homologues suggest that they may represent additional wheat CBF genes. To compare wheat CBFs with the closely related Triticeae species barley, we included 13 of the 19 HvCBF genes reported (Table 2) (Skinner et al. 2005). The two additional pseudogenes, HvCBFIIIc-8B and HvCBFIIIc-8C, and the remaining four genes HvCBFIIIc-10B (97.2%), HvCBFIVa-2B (98.8%), HvCBFIVd-4B (99.8%) and HvCBFIVd-4D (96.4%) were not included since their high homologies with their closest related paralog suggested that these duplications happened following the divergence of wheat and barley. Several other CBF sequences from families of the monocotyledon order Poales were included in the analysis (Table 2). In addition, CBF representatives of two other monocotyledon orders Arecales and Zingiberales were also included in the analysis (Table 2). HvCBF7 and TmCBF7 (Skinner et al. 2005; Miller et al. 2006) were not included in this phylogenetic analysis since their protein sequence contains a less conserved CBF signature and the presence of motifs found in subgroup IIId of ERF proteins (Nakano et al. 2006).
###end p 49

###xml 44 45 44 45 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig1" ref-type="fig">1</xref>

###xml 3010 3025 <span type="species:ncbi:4513">Hordeum vulgare</span>
###xml 3030 3044 <span type="species:ncbi:4522">Lolium perenne</span>
###xml 3049 3061 <span type="species:ncbi:4530">Oryza sativa</span>
###xml 3066 3082 <span type="species:ncbi:38727">Panicum virgatum</span>
###xml 3087 3109 <span type="species:ncbi:115513">Rhapidophyllum hystrix</span>
###xml 3114 3121 <span type="species:ncbi:4558">Sorghum</span>
###xml 3135 3149 <span type="species:ncbi:4550">Secale cereale</span>
###xml 3154 3175 <span type="species:ncbi:4547">Saccharum officinarum</span>
###xml 3180 3194 <span type="species:ncbi:54447">Sabal palmetto</span>
###xml 3199 3216 <span type="species:ncbi:4565">Triticum aestivum</span>
###xml 3221 3240 <span type="species:ncbi:4568">Triticum monococcum</span>
###xml 3245 3253 <span type="species:ncbi:4577">Zea mays</span>
###xml 3258 3277 <span type="species:ncbi:94328">Zingiber officinale</span>
The phylogenetic analysis presented in Fig. 1 shows that monocot CBFs cluster into several distinct monophyletic groups. Observation of the first group (named CBFI) reveals three distinct branches. The first branch containing the CBF sequences from the Arecales and Zingiberales orders (SpCBFI, RhCBFI, DlCBFI and ZoCBFI) is separated from the two branches containing Poales CBF sequences. As additional CBF genes are identified and characterized in other monocotyledon plants, it may reveal functional differences that will necessitate a classification as a distinct subgroup to better reflect their evolutionary relationships. The second branch contains the lone rice gene OsCBFI-1F. If orthologs of this gene are found in other Poales members, this will support the need to define a separate subgroup representing these more distantly clustered genes. The remaining branch, tentatively named CBFIa, contains sequences from the Poales/Poaceae subfamilies Oryzaceae, Panicoideae and Pooideae suggesting that the ancestral Poaceae CBFIa was present before divergence of these subfamilies, and that none of these families have lost this CBF gene. In fact, Oryzaceae and Pooideae may have already possessed two genes before divergence since rice and barley each have a pair of genes (Fig. 1). Although only TaCBFIa-A11 has been identified to date, the above information suggests that wheat could have one or two additional genes (orthologs of CBFIa-1 and OsCBFI-1F). The characteristic of group CBFI is that it is the only one that contains CBF genes from the three orders Poales, Arecales and Zingiberales. Although the identification of different CBF genes from orders other than Poales is still limited, their clustering with the CBFI group suggests that it is the most ancient group in monocots. In support of this, proteins encoded by CBFI genes are the ones that show the highest homologies with dicotyledonous CBF proteins suggestive of their closer evolutionary relationship with an ancestral type CBF. This was also noted previously by Skinner et al. (2005). Fig. 1Phylogenetic relationships between monocot CBF genes. The nucleotide sequence corresponding to the AP2 DNA binding domain and the conserved flanking signature sequences PKK/RPAGRxKFxETRHP and DSAWR defined by Jaglo et al. (2001) were aligned using ClustalW and manually adjusted. An unrooted Minimum Evolution tree was derived from this alignment using the Kimura 2-parameter. The CBF nomenclature used is described in Table 2. CBF genes belonging to specific monophyletic groups are contoured. Complete CBF names are shown in red for wheat species, in blue for barley, green for rice and black for all other species examined. Groups lightly shaded contain only Pooideae sequences. TmCBFIVd-4 was included in group IVd based on the analysis of the complete sequence. Ac Agrostis capillaries, As Avena sativa, Ast Agrostis stolonifera, Bd Brachypodium distachyon, Dl Dypsis lutescens, Fa Festuca arundinacea, Hb Hordeum brevisubulatum, Hv Hordeum vulgare, Lp Lolium perenne, Os Oryza sativa, Pv Panicum virgatum, Rh Rhapidophyllum hystrix, Sb Sorghum bicolour, Sc Secale cereale, So Saccharum officinarum, Sp Sabal palmetto, Ta Triticum aestivum, Tm Triticum monococcum, Zm Zea mays, Zo Zingiber officinale
###end p 50
